Pantomimetic Origin Of LanguageOpen sidebarPantomimetic Origin Of LanguageChatGPT 4YouDresherInvasion of the language virusesB. Elan DresherUniversity of TorontoOriginally   published   in Glot   International 4,2,page   12, February 1999.Time was when one could write a book about language learning or the evolution of language without taking into account the linguistic theories of the day. This has been changing, thanks to the work of Noam Chomsky over the past forty years, and to writers like Ray Jackendoff (1994), David Lightfoot (1982), and Steven Pinker (1994), whose books have established the relevance of linguistic theory  to  mainstream  discussions  of  human  psychology  and  the  nature  of  the  mind.  Judging  from  a recent book by Terrence Deacon, however, there is still a long way to go before the actual contents of linguistic  research  penetrate  beyond  the  circle  of  its  practitioners  and  become  common  currency  in the wider intellectual culture.Deaconâs The symbolic species: The co-evolution of language and the brain is an impressive tour de force through neuroscience, ethology, palaeontology, anthropology, evolutionary theory, and much  else.  The  blurbs  on  the  back  cover  proclaim  this  to  be  a  âsuperb  and  innovative  look  at  the evolution of languageâ and âthe best book yet written on the evolution of language.â âThe Symbolic Species should transform the foundations of the human sciences.âRalph   L.   Holloway,   an anthropologist who has reviewed the book in American Scientist, writes that it is âa nonpareilâ, âjust leaps and leaps above other evolution-of-language books published during thepast five years.âDespite its brilliance, Holloway imagines that there will be parts âpicked at by linguistsâ, and no  wonder.  Before  we  have  even  opened  the  book  we  read  that  âDeacon  has  mounted  a  serious challenge  to  the  neo-Chomskians...This is theoretical dynamite, planted deep under the walls of  the neo-Chomskian  fortress.â  The  picking,  not  to  mention  the  hurling  down  of  arrows,  stones,  and burning oil, is well underway in Derek Bickertonâs review in New Scientist. Needless to say, it will continue here.Deaconâs book is divided into three parts. The first part, to which we will return shortly, is on language.  Part  two  is  full  of  interesting  information  on  the  brain,  including  a  survey  of  the  neural bases of language and speech. Part three, âCo-Evolution,â is quite literally science fiction in the strict sense  of  the  term,  in  which  Deacon  advances  a  scenario  for  how  the  transition  to  language  and symbolic reference might have occurred in early hominid communities.With  respect  to  language,  Deacon  (102  ff.)  accepts  the  claims  of  linguists  that  language learning presents a very difficult problem, and he presents a respectable version of the argument from the poverty of the stimulus that childrenâs experience does not suffice to account for their acquisition of language. Nevertheless, he believes that âinnate Universal Grammar is a cure that is more drastic than the disease.â Though he agrees that âhuman brains come into the world specially equippedâto acquire language, he rejects the âpreformationistâ interpretation of innateness as involving an innate âlanguage competenceâ, or ârules in the brain.âIf  neither  experience  nor  innate  principles  explain  language  acquisition,  where  else  can  the required  grammatical  knowledge  be?  Deacon  proposes  (105)  that  âthe  extra  support  for language learning  is  vested  neither  in  the  brain  of  the  child  nor  in  the  brains  of  parents  and  teachers,  but outside brains, in language itself.â In other words (109), âChildrenâs minds need not innately embody language structures, if languages embody the predispositions of childrenâs minds!â
B.ELAN DRESHER2Why  did  nobody  think  of  this  before?  Perhaps  because  this  position  appears  to  have untenable,  not  to  say  absurd,  consequences.  Deacon  is  undeterred;  languages  are  like  living organisms that must be studied in evolutionary terms. He goes on (111): âIn some ways it is helpful to  imagine  language  as  an  independent  life  form  that  colonizes  and  parasitizes  human  brains,  using them  to  reproduce.â  Languages  âmight  better  be  compared  to  viruses...[which  are]  minimally packaged strings of DNA or RNA.â Deacon invokes the name of August Schleicher, an important nineteenth century linguist who viewed  languages  as  natural  organisms,  like  plants  or  animals.  As  Lightfoot  (1999:  35,  227)  points out, Schleicher (1863) was influenced in this regard by Darwin. Deacon takes up Darwinâs idea that languages  tend  to  change  under  pressure  of  natural  selection  in  the  direction  of having shorter, âeasierâ  forms  (Darwin  1874:  88â92).  Deacon  stresses  (109):  âLanguages  donât  just  change,  they evolve...Languages  are  under  powerful  selection  pressure  to  fit  childrenâs  likely guessesâ [emphasis in  originalâBED].  Thus,  languages  are  easy  for  childrento  learn  because  they  evolved  in  such  a way hat  they  match  childrenâs  biases.  âThe  key  to  understanding language  learnability...lies in...language changeâ (115).David  Lightfootâs  new  book, The  development  of  language:  Acquisition,  change,  and evolution, sets out to show that this viewâthat the principles of language are to be sought in a theory of changeâis a major fallacy that has deep roots in the nineteenth century, the âcentury of historyâ,and  continues  to  influence  some  linguistic  research  to  this  day.  This  view  is  mistaken  because languages  are  not  plants  or  animals,  or  even  viruses.  They  have  no  DNA, they do not pass through fixed stages of development from infancy to death. Even asserting that âtheyâ do not do these things is not quite correct: there is simply no âtheyâ there that can have these properties.Moreover,  contrary  to  Deaconâs  repeated  assertions,  there  is  not  a  shred  of  evidence  from language change that languages evolve. The language faculty may well have evolved, and for all we know  languages  at  some  distant  prehistoric  stage  may  have  been  different  from  modern  languages. However,  we  cannot  reach  any  such  hypothesized  pre-languages  through  the  study  of  language change or the reconstruction of any ancient or proto-language accessible to us.One  might  well  wonder  what  it  is  that  Deacon  finds  so  unacceptable  about  the  notion  of innate  universal  grammar  (UG)  that  he  would  rather  leap  into  this  particular  abyss.  Indeed,  what  is the  difference  between  endowing  children  with  UG  and  attributing  to  them  certain  biases  or predispositions  to  guess?  Deacon  writes  with  such  authority  about  so  many  technical  subjects  that one is reluctant to say this, but there is a hollow space at the linguistic core of this book. In the places where concrete linguistic examples ought to be, Deacon gives us analogies and parables: Languages are  like  user-friendly  Macintosh  computers  that  are  easy  to  operate;  or  a  language  is  like  a  rigged Roulette wheel that consistently lands on numbers that an unwitting gambler tends to bet on.In  contrast  to  his  discussion  of  the  brain,  which  is  admirably  clear  and  precise,  Deaconâs references to grammatical principles are extremely vague. According to him, the theory of UG posits âaxiomatic  rule  systemsâ  that specify  âgrammatical  operationsâ, and invariant âdeep structuresâ or âdeep  grammatical  logic.â  Deacon  argues  (333)  that  these  âdeepâ  principles  of  UG  are  highly variable  at  the  surface,  and  so  they  could  not  have  evolved  specific  neural  supports,  and  âare ineligible to participate in Baldwinian evolution!âElsewhere,  however,  he  suggests  (339)  that  âthe  best  candidates  for  innate language adaptations   turn   out   to   be   some   very   general   structural   characteristics   of...speech,   and   the computational  demands  this  medium  imposes  when  it  comes  to  symbolic  analysis.â  But  many proposed  principles  of  UG  have  a  computational  and  structural  character,  and  they  may  well  be associated  with  invariant  (though  not  necessarily  transparent  at  the  surface)  cues.  Thus,  even  if  we agree  that  every  aspect  of  UG  must  be  adaptive,  as  required  by  Baldwinian  selection  (but  see Lightfoot 1999: 243f. for some UG conditions that may be maladaptive), we must reserve judgement about whether UG could have evolved until we have put some concrete examples of the table.

Here's a simplified breakdown of the key points and claims:Impact of Noam Chomsky: Dresher acknowledges the significant influence of Noam Chomsky's work in linguistics over the past decades, especially in shaping discussions about human psychology and the nature of the mind.Critique of Terrence Deacon's Book: Dresher critically reviews Terrence Deacon's book "The Symbolic Species," which explores the co-evolution of language and the human brain. While acknowledging the book's ambitious scope, Dresher points out its shortcomings, especially in its approach to language evolution.Debate on Language Evolution: A central issue in the excerpt is Deacon's claim that languages evolve in a way similar to living organisms or viruses. Deacon suggests that languages adapt to fit children's cognitive biases, making them easier to learn over time. This view challenges the traditional linguistic theory of Universal Grammar (UG) proposed by Chomsky.Universal Grammar (UG) vs. External Linguistic Influences: Universal Grammar is a theory that suggests humans are born with an innate understanding of the basic principles of language. Deacon, however, argues against this idea of an innate language competence. Instead, he proposes that the "extra support" for language learning exists not in the brain but in language itself.Language as a Living Organism: Deacon uses analogies to describe languages as independent life forms that colonize human brains, comparing them to viruses. This metaphorical description is used to argue that languages change and evolve independently of human brains.Critique of Deacon's Approach: Dresher critiques Deacon for lacking concrete linguistic examples and for his vague references to grammatical principles. Dresher argues that Deacon's comparisons (like languages to viruses) are more metaphorical and lack empirical evidence.In summary, Dresher's critique centers on the debate between viewing language as an innate human ability (as per Universal Grammar) versus viewing it as an external, evolving entity, akin to a virus, that adapts to human cognition. Just like organisms, languages are considered to evolve, grow, adapt, and even 'die' over time. This perspective allows linguists to study languages using principles similar to those used in biology, particularly evolutionary biology.August Schleicher's Contribution: August Schleicher, a linguist from the 19th century, is particularly noted for applying this analogy in a systematic way. He proposed that languages could be studied like organisms, with their own "family trees." This approach led to the development of methods for tracing the evolution of languages by examining common inherited traits, similar to how biologists trace the phylogenetic trees of species.Use in Comparative Linguistics: In comparative linguistics, this analogy has been useful for understanding how languages change over time. By examining languages as if they were organisms, linguists can identify patterns of change, divergence, and convergence, much like biologists study speciation and evolutionary relationships among species.Understanding Language Change: The organism analogy has been a heuristic tool â a way of thinking about languages that guides research and hypothesis formation. His work continues to contribute to our understanding of the complex mechanisms underlying language and cognition.YouWhat predicts individual differences in childrenâs acquisition of consonant production across languages? Considerations of childrenâs development of early speech production have traditionally emphasized inherent physiological constraints of the vocal apparatus that speakers generally have in common (i.e., articulatory complexity). In contrast, we propose a statistical learning account of phonological development, in which phonological regularities of the ambient language guide childrenâs learning of those regularities in production. Across four languages (English, Spanish, Japanese, and Korean), we utilized recent meta-analytic dataset of age of consonant acquisition spanning 28 studies. High-density measures of children's ambient language environment from over 8,000 transcripts of speech directed to over 1,000 children were used to assess how well the frequency of consonants in child-directed speech predict the age of consonant acquisition. Our results suggest that both frequency and articulatory complexity are related to age of acquisition, with similar results found for English, Spanish, Japanese, and Korean. Consonants heard frequently by children tended to be incorporated into their production repertoires earlier and consonants heard less frequently are incorporated into production repertoires later in development. We discuss future directions that incorporate a statistical learning pathway towards learning to produce the sound patterns of the ambient language.

Contreras Kallens, P., Elmlinger, S., Wang, K., Goldstein, M., McLeod, S., Crowe, K., & Christiansen, M. (2023). Statistical learning or phonological universals? Ambient language statistics guide consonant acquisition in four languages. Proceedings of the Annual Meeting of the Cognitive Science Society, 45. Retrieved from https://escholarship.org/uc/item/3wj6767pChatGPTChatGPTThe study by Contreras Kallens, Elmlinger, Wang, Goldstein, McLeod, Crowe, and Christiansen (2023) examines the factors influencing children's acquisition of consonant production across multiple languages. Here are the key points and findings from the study:Traditional View vs. Statistical Learning Account: Traditionally, the development of speech production in children has been thought to be primarily influenced by the physiological constraints of the vocal apparatus, such as articulatory complexity. However, this study proposes a statistical learning approach, suggesting that the phonological regularities of the ambient language significantly guide childrenâs learning of these regularities in production.Cross-Language Study: The research spans four languages: English, Spanish, Japanese, and Korean. It uses a comprehensive meta-analytic dataset covering the age of consonant acquisition across 28 studies.Data Analysis: The study analyzed high-density measures of children's ambient language environment. This involved over 8,000 transcripts of speech directed to over 1,000 children to assess how the frequency of consonants in child-directed speech predicts the age of consonant acquisition.Findings: The results indicated that both the frequency of consonants in the ambient language and articulatory complexity are related to the age at which children acquire these consonants. Specifically, consonants that are heard more frequently by children tend to be incorporated into their production repertoires earlier, while those heard less frequently are incorporated later.Consistency Across Languages: Interestingly, similar patterns were found across all four languages studied (English, Spanish, Japanese, and Korean), suggesting a common underlying mechanism in consonant acquisition across different linguistic backgrounds.Implications for Phonological Development: These findings support the idea that childrenâs acquisition of speech sounds is not solely dependent on physiological factors. Instead, it highlights the role of statistical learning, where children pick up on the frequency and patterns of sounds in their environment.Future Directions: The study suggests further research incorporating a statistical learning pathway to understand better how children learn to produce the sound patterns of their ambient language.This research contributes significantly to our understanding of phonological development in children, emphasizing the importance of the language environment and exposure in the acquisition of speech sounds. It provides evidence for the statistical learning model in language acquisition, showing that childrenâs exposure to language shapes their speech development.YouHow do children learn to produce the consonant sounds of 
their ambient language? Traditional approaches have focused 
on the difficulty with which consonants are produced by 
speakers (Jakobson, 1941). This difficulty has been 
conceptualized as the articulatory complexity involved with 
producing a given consonant (Kent, 1992). A recent meta- 
analysis of children's consonant acquisition trajectories 
across 27 languages suggests that while some stability in 
consonant acquisition over development exists, there is also 
wide variation in consonant acquisition trajectories across 
and within languages, suggesting that consonant acquisition 
is not exclusively about articulatory complexity (McLeod & 
Crowe, 2018). For example, the consonant /f/ (as in fork) is 
relatively stable across and within languages (i.e., is 
characterized by low variance in its age of acquisition across 
languages), but the learning of /v/ (as in vase) appears to be 
guided more so by the extent to which children perceive its 
use in the ambient language (Crowe & McLeod, 2020; 
McLeod & Crowe, 2018). 
Another source of variation in childrenâs consonant 
acquisition may derive from the distribution of different 
consonants in the ambient language. Statistical learningâ 
sensitivity to distributional patterns in the speech 
environmentâhas been a productive alternative research 
program to universalist claims of language acquisition (Frost, 
Armstrong, & Christiansen, 2019). Several lines of evidence 
suggest that infant vocal learning, including learning to 
produce consonant sounds, requires perceptual mechanisms 
to incorporate patterns of the ambient language into more 
mature vocalizations (Boysson-Bardies et al., 1991; Edwards 
et al., 2015; Edwards & Beckman, 2008; Goldstein & 
Schwade, 2008; Ingram, 1988). For example, compared to 
German-learning infants, Nso-learning infants produce 
significantly more click-like consonant sounds, a consonant 
pattern found mainly in the learning environment of Nso- 
learning infants (Wermke et al., 2013). 
There are at least three reasons underlying the limited 
progress on fully understanding ambient language effects on 
consonant production learning in children. First, substantial 
quantifiable data on children's language environment has 
only recently become widely available to researchers 
(MacWhinney, 2000; Sanchez et al., 2019). Past consonant 
acquisition research was not able gain insights into the 
perceptual regularities present in child-directed speech, the 
sounds that children are most likely to encounter in their 
everyday learning environments. Additionally, access to 
high-density cross-linguistic data on children's consonant 
acquisition trajectories has only recently become possible 
(McLeod & Crowe, 2018). 
Second, previous models of articulatory complexity (AC) 
are themselves based on age of acquisition data (Kent, 1992). 
For example, Kent (1992) based his analysis of articulatory 
complexity on previous documentation of age of acquisition 
of consonants found by Sander (1972) and Dinnsen et al. 
(1990). AC classifications were inferred from the observed 
order in which English-learning infants and children learned 
to produce consonants. Historically, the directionality of this 
inference has been overlooked (i.e., age of consonant 
acquisition â articulatory complexity), and findings have 
been interpreted with the assumption that articulatory

It contrasts two main theories: traditional approaches focusing on articulatory complexity and newer approaches based on statistical learning. Here are the key aspects of the study:Traditional Approaches (Articulatory Complexity):Historically, the acquisition of consonant sounds by children has been attributed to the physical difficulty or articulatory complexity involved in producing these sounds.This view suggests that some consonants are inherently more challenging to produce due to the physiological constraints of the vocal apparatus.Statistical Learning Approach:This approach posits that childrenâs acquisition of consonants is influenced by the frequency and distribution of these sounds in their ambient language.It focuses on how children are sensitive to the patterns and regularities in the speech they hear, and how this exposure shapes their ability to produce consonants.Evidence Against Articulatory Complexity Alone:Research, including a meta-analysis by McLeod & Crowe (2018), indicates that while there is some consistency in the order of consonant acquisition across languages, there's also considerable variation. In all languages complex consonant structures and sounds with difficult articulatory complexity are acquired later. It is called markedness because the existence of the sound th for example implies the mastery of the sound t, this also is reflected in the order second language speakers?ChatGPTChatGPTThe concept of phonological markedness and the statistical learning approach to consonant acquisition, while related, focus on different aspects of language learning:Phonological Markedness:Phonological markedness is a theoretical concept in linguistics that refers to the relative "simplicity" or "complexity" of phonological elements (like sounds) in a language.In this theory, "unmarked" sounds are those that are more common, simpler to articulate, and typically acquired earlier by children. These are considered the default or basic forms in a language."Marked" sounds are less common, more complex, and usually acquired later. They often depend on the acquisition of their unmarked counterparts. For example, the sound "th" (as in "think") might be considered marked compared to the simpler "t" sound.In second language acquisition, learners often acquire unmarked sounds more easily than marked ones, reflecting this inherent complexity.Statistical Learning Approach:The statistical learning approach emphasizes the role of exposure and frequency in the ambient language. It suggests that children learn to produce sounds based on how often they hear these sounds in their environment.This approach does not necessarily contradict the concept of markedness but adds an additional layer of explanation. It suggests that the frequency of exposure to certain sounds in a child's environment can influence the order and ease of acquisition.For instance, a sound that is complex (marked) but very frequent in a child's ambient language might be acquired earlier than expected under traditional markedness theory.Differences:Phonological markedness is rooted in the inherent properties of sounds (like articulatory complexity) and their relative frequency and commonality across languages.The statistical learning approach focuses more on the individual childâs experience and exposure within their specific language environment. It suggests that variation in acquisition can be due to differences in exposure to specific sounds.Markedness provides a more universal, cross-linguistic framework, while statistical learning is more about individual and language-specific experiences.In summary, phonological markedness deals with the inherent properties of sounds and their relative complexity across languages. In contrast, the statistical learning approach focuses on how individual exposure and frequency in the ambient language influence the acquisition of these sounds. Both approaches offer valuable insights, and they can complement each other in understanding language acquisition.YouAnother related concept is the observations that babies babble by waving their arms and randomly making sounds, which involve the making the possible sounds and gestures of language. Let's delve into these briefly:

1. **Bow-Wow Theory:** This is one of the proposed theories for the origin of language, suggesting that human speech began as imitations of natural sounds - like animal calls. Over time, these imitations became symbolic and then abstracted into more complex forms of language.

2. **Arnie Cox's Mimetic Music and Embodied Cognition:** Arnie Cox has discussed the idea of musical meaning arising from embodied interactions with musical sounds. Essentially, our understanding and perception of music is rooted in physical actions, gestures, and our bodily experiences.

3. **Shanker & Greenspan's First Idea:** This relates to the developmental processes of children and the importance of emotional and relational interactions in the early stages of life. Such interactions are crucial for cognitive and linguistic development.

Considering the above and extending it to Neanderthals:

- Neanderthals lived in complex social groups, used tools, and had symbolic behaviors (e.g., the possible use of pigments and burial practices). If they were also mimicking sounds from nature or employing embodied cognition principles, this could have laid the groundwork for some form of structured communication.

- Recent evidence suggests that Neanderthals might have had the physical capacity for speech. Their hyoid bone (related to tongue movement and speech production) is similar to that of modern humans. Also, genetic studies show that they had the FOXP2 gene, which is linked to speech and language.

- If Neanderthals were indeed using biomimetic strategies like mimicking animal sounds or other natural phenomena, it would imply a level of cognitive sophistication. This could further suggest that they were not just mimicking for survival (like hunting) but possibly for more complex social interactions.

To integrate these concepts into a comprehensive theory of Neanderthal communication, one would need to explore archaeological evidence, cognitive science studies, and linguistic theories. It's an exciting area of study that could shed more light on our shared evolutionary past and the origins of language and cognition.

Certainly! The "Stone Piano Theory" is a hypothesis that Neanderthals might have created musical instruments using stones. The idea is based on the discovery of lithophones, or musical stones, which when struck, produce tonal sounds. These instruments may have been a precursor to other musical instruments and played a role in the development of Neanderthal culture.

Integrating the Biomimetic Origin Of Language Evolution (BOOLE) concept with the Stone Piano Theory can be approached in several ways:

1. **Mimicry of Natural Sounds:** Just as the "bow-wow theory" posits that humans imitated animal sounds, Neanderthals might have mimicked sounds from their environment using stone instruments. By hitting or striking stones in specific ways, they could replicate the sounds of animals, water, wind, and more. This sound mimicry could have served both practical purposes (like hunting) and social or symbolic functions.

2. **Embodied Cognition and Rhythm:** Building on Arnie Cox's idea of embodied cognition, the act of creating music or rhythms with stones could be a form of non-verbal communication. The rhythmic patterns might have been a physical manifestation of internal emotions, thoughts, or experiences, helping Neanderthals connect with each other.

3. **Emotional and Relational Interactions:** Drawing from Shanker & Greenspan's concept, the act of producing and sharing music could have fostered deeper emotional and relational bonds within Neanderthal communities. Music, as an expressive medium, could have played a role in group cohesion, rituals, or storytelling.

4. **Evolution of Communication:** The combination of mimicking natural sounds with the stone piano and using rhythmic patterns might have been a stepping stone toward more complex forms of communication. By associating specific sounds or rhythms with meanings or stories, Neanderthals might have laid the foundation for a primitive form of language.ChatGPTChatGPTYour integration of these ideas into a hypothesis about Neanderthal communication is fascinating and aligns with interdisciplinary approaches in understanding the evolution of language and cognition.
